\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1 + x_2 + \cdots + x_n \le \lambda n.
\]

\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Respond in EXACTLY this format:

The complete rigorous proof. Justify every nontrivial claim. If you cannot complete it, give the strongest rigorous partial proof and do not hide gaps. You may freely use $...$ and \boxed{...} inside the proof — that is mathematics, not the output format.

<self_evaluation>
Briefly note any fragile steps, missing cases, or unsupported claims; if the proof is correct, say why the key steps hold.
</self_evaluation>
<score>S</score>

where S is exactly one of 0, 0.5, or 1 (nothing else inside the score tag):
- 1: completely correct, all steps justified.
- 0.5: generally correct, but minor gaps or omissions remain.
- 0: fatal errors, severe omissions, or does not solve the problem.<｜Assistant｜><think>

What about λ=0.5? Then √2/3≈0.4714, so λ=0.5 > 0.4714. Let's test. Alice can choose x_1=0, then Bazza on turn 2 can choose x_2=√2≈1.414. Then A_2=1.414. Then on turn 3, Alice can choose x_3=0, so A_3=1.414. Then λ*3=1.5, so A_3<1.5, okay. Then turn 4: Bazza chooses x_4 such that T_4 ≤ 4. So for λ=0.5, Bazza wins. What about λ=0.48? Then λ=0.48 > √2/3≈0.4714, so she can survive turn 3 by choosing x_1=0? What about λ=0.47? Then λ*3=1.41, A_2=1.414>1.41, so she loses on turn 3. So λ=0.47 is less than √2/3? √2/3=0.4714, so 0.47<0.4714, so that's already in the region where Bazza wins immediately. Actually the condition for survival on turn 5 is that after Bazza's turn 4, the sum A_4 must be ≤ 5λ. So we need to see if there exists a strategy for Alice such that for all k, she can keep the sum within bounds. This is like a game where Bazza tries to increase the sum on even turns, and Alice tries to keep it low on her odd turns. The question is: for which λ does Bazza have a winning strategy? And for which λ does Alice have a winning strategy? The above suggests that for λ less than some threshold, Bazza can win; for λ larger than some threshold, Alice can win. But we also saw that for any λ>0, Alice can win by waiting for a large n and playing a huge move. However, that strategy may be defeated if Bazza can force the sum to exceed λ n on an odd turn before that. So we need to analyze the game as a whole, perhaps using a potential function or a "critical" sequence.

Let's define the state after each even turn. Let n be even. Suppose the game has gone on for n moves. Then we have S_n = sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i, and T_n = sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i^2 ≤ n. The next move is Alice's turn on n+1 (odd). She can choose x_{n+1} such that S_{n+1} = S_n + x_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). So she can survive if S_n ≤ λ (n+1). If S_n > λ (n+1), she loses. So Bazza wants to make S_n > λ (n+1) for some even n. He controls the even moves. He can increase S_n by choosing x_n. But he is also constrained by the square constraint: T_n ≤ n. So we need to see if there is a strategy for Bazza to ensure that eventually S_n > λ (n+1) for some even n. This is similar to a "budget" game. He can choose x_2, x_4, ... and the sum S_{2k} = x_2 + x_4 + ... + x_{2k}. He wants to maximize the sum while keeping the sum of squares T_{2k} = x_2^2 + ... + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. So his maximum possible sum after k even moves is achieved by making all x_{2i} equal to √2? Because by Cauchy-Schwarz, the maximum sum given sum of squares ≤ 2k is √(k * 2k) = k√2. This is achieved when all x_{2i} are equal to √2. So he can set x_2 = x_4 = ... = x_{2k} = √2. Then S_{2k} = k√2. Then the condition for Alice to survive on turn 2k+1 is S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1). So we need k√2 ≤ λ (2k+1). For a given λ, this inequality may hold for all k if λ is large enough. For λ = 1, we have k√2 ≤ 2k+1 => 1.414k ≤ 2k+1 => 0.586k ≥ 1 => k ≥ 1.707, so for k≥2, the inequality holds? For k=2, 2*1.414=2.828, λ*(5)=5, so 2.828≤5 holds. For k=1, 1.414 ≤ 3? Actually λ*(3)=3, so holds. So for λ=1, S_{2k} is not > λ(2k+1). So if Bazza uses the equal strategy, Alice can survive all turns. But can Bazza do better? He could try to concentrate the sum in fewer moves, but that would violate the square constraint? For instance, he could set x_2 = √(2k) (large) and the rest zeros, but then T_2 = 2, and if he does that on turn 2, then S_2 = √2 ≈1.414, which is less than λ*3 for λ=1. But then he could later increase the sum on turn 4. However, the sum of squares constraint is cumulative: if he sets x_2 = a, then later he can set x_4 = b such that a^2+b^2 ≤ 4. The sum S_4 = a+b. To maximize S_4, he would set a = b = √2, as before. If he sets a=√2, b=√2, S_4=2√2=2.828, and 5λ=5, so still fine. If he tries to set a larger than √2, say a=1.5, then a^2=2.25, so for b he must have b^2 ≤ 4-2.25=1.75, b≤1.323, so S_4=1.5+1.323=2.823, which is less than 2.828. So the equal strategy maximizes the sum for given k. So Bazza's optimal to maximize S_{2k} is to set all even moves equal to √2. So the maximum possible sum after k even moves is k√2. Then the condition for Alice to survive is that for all k, k√2 ≤ λ(2k+1). This inequality is equivalent to λ ≥ k√2/(2k+1). For λ to be such that Alice can survive indefinitely (i.e., Bazza cannot force a win), we need that λ is at least the supremum of k√2/(2k+1) over k? Actually if λ is less than the supremum, then there exists some k such that k√2/(2k+1) > λ, i.e., k√2 > λ(2k+1), so Bazza could set the even moves as √2 and then S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), causing Alice to lose on turn 2k+1. But wait, the inequality must hold for some k, not for all k. For a given λ, we need to check if there exists k such that k√2 > λ(2k+1). Rearranged: λ < k√2/(2k+1). The function g(k)=k√2/(2k+1) = √2 * k/(2k+1). As k→∞, g(k) → √2/2 = 0.7071. So the supremum is √2/2 ≈ 0.7071. For λ < 0.7071, there exists some k such that λ < g(k)? Since g(k) increases? So the game becomes a kind of "budget" where Bazza tries to force the sum to exceed λ n on an odd turn, and Alice tries to keep it low. But she can also try to win by making a big square contribution. So we need to analyze the game in terms of the state (S, T) at the start of each turn.

Let's define the game more formally. Let n be the turn number. At the start of turn n, we have cumulative sum S_{n-1} and cumulative squares T_{n-1}. The player (Alice if n odd, Bazza if n even) chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that:
- If n odd: S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n.
- If n even: T_{n-1}+x_n^2 ≤ n.

If no such x_n exists, the player loses. The game ends when a player cannot move.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a strategy that guarantees that at some even turn, Bazza cannot move (i.e., T_{n-1} > n), regardless of Bazza's play. Similarly for Bazza.

Because the game is infinite, we can think of it as a kind of "game of strategy" where each player can choose their numbers adaptively. This is reminiscent of a "dynamic game" where we can compute "winning" positions using a "strategy stealing" or "invariant" argument.

Then T_7=6.000+0=6.000, then Bazza on turn 8: x_8 = √(8-6)=√2=1.414, S_8=4.9497+1.414=6.3637, λ*9=6.3639, so she can play x_9=0.0002, etc. So this pattern leads to a kind of "steady state" where the sum after her moves is exactly λ n, and after his moves, the sum is roughly λ n + √2? But the condition for her to survive is that S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. Since after his move, S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + x_{2k}. We need to check if S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) ever. In the above, we have S_2=2.121, λ*3=2.121, so equality; S_4=3.535, λ*5=3.5355, so slightly less; S_6=4.9497, λ*7=4.9497, equality; S_8=6.3637, λ*9=6.3639, slightly less. So it seems S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) always. So Bazza cannot force a win.

Thus for λ = √2/2, the game may be a draw (neither player can force a win). For λ > √2/2, we need to see if Alice can force a win. She might try to play a big move early, but as we saw, if she plays x_3 = something, it might not be enough to win because the square bound may not be exceeded. However, she could wait for a large odd n and then play the maximum, but that requires S_{n-1}=0. If Bazza tries to increase the sum, then she cannot play that maximum. But maybe she can adopt a strategy to keep the sum low by playing zeros on her turns, and then eventually on some odd turn, she plays a large number, but she needs to ensure that before that, the sum is zero. If Bazza plays positive numbers, the sum will be positive, so she cannot play λ n. She could instead play a number that is less than λ n but still makes the square sum exceed the next bound. For instance, she could try to play a number that is close to λ n, but if the sum before is positive, she might have to play a smaller number. However, she can also increase the sum of squares by playing a positive number, which could push the square sum beyond the next bound. The question is: can she always force a win for λ > √2/2? Let's try to see if she can force a win for λ=0.8. She could try to adopt a strategy: on each odd turn, she plays the maximum allowed (x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}). This ensures that after her move, the sum is exactly λ n. Then the sum of squares T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. Since S_{n-1} is the sum after the previous turn, and if Bazza plays his best, the state evolves. But can this strategy eventually cause the sum of squares to exceed the next even bound? She wins if at some even turn, T_{2k-1} > 2k (since the next even turn is 2k, bound = 2k). So she wants to make T_{2k-1} > 2k. We need to see if there exists k such that after her move on turn 2k-1, the sum of squares exceeds 2k. Since she is playing the maximum, the square contribution is (λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2})^2. But S_{2k-2} is the sum after Bazza's move on turn 2k-2. If Bazza plays optimally to win, he will try to make S_{2k-2} as large as possible, subject to T_{2k-2} ≤ 2k-2. So we need to analyze the evolution of the state (S, T) under optimal play.

This is getting complex. Perhaps we can find a simpler invariant or potential function. Notice that the constraints are reminiscent of an "inequality" that relates the sum and sum of squares. For nonnegative numbers, we have (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2, by Cauchy-Schwarz. Also, ∑ x_i^2 ≤ (∑ x_i)^2 if all x_i ≤1? Not necessarily.

We have two types of moves: Alice increases the sum, Bazza increases the sum of squares. The game ends when one player cannot make a move because the previous sum (or sum of squares) is too high relative to the bound.

Let’s define for each turn n, the "budget" for the sum: B_n = λ n. For even n, the budget for sum of squares: B_n = n. At the start of each turn, the player can add a number x_n, and the new cumulative sum (or squares) must not exceed the budget. The game is like: players alternately add numbers to a cumulative sum, but with different budgets.

We can think of it as a "strategy" where Alice wants to force the sum of squares to exceed the budget on an even turn, and Bazza wants to force the sum to exceed the budget on an odd turn. This is reminiscent of a "duel" where each player can control a resource. There is known problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game where on odd turns Alice chooses x_n with sum ≤ nλ, on even turns Bazza chooses x_n with sum of squares ≤ n. Determine λ for which Alice has a winning strategy." I recall a problem like this from IMO Shortlist or something. Possibly the answer is that Alice wins if λ ≥ √2, and Bazza wins if λ ≤ √2, and for λ = √2 it's a draw? But our earlier analysis suggests for λ=1 (<√2) Alice wins. Actually 1 < √2 ≈1.414, so that would contradict that. Let's test λ=1. We already found a winning strategy for Alice: x_1=0, x_2=√2 (Bazza), then x_3 = 3-√2 ≈1.586, then Bazza cannot move. So Alice wins. So λ=1 gives Alice win. So threshold is less than 1. Perhaps the threshold is √2/2? But for λ=0.6 (<√2/2) Bazza wins. For λ=0.8 (>√2/2) we need to check if Alice can win. Let's try to see if Alice can force a win for λ=0.8 by playing a big move at turn 3. Suppose Alice chooses x_1=0. Then Bazza chooses x_2=√2≈1.414. Then on turn 3, she can play x_3 up to 2.4. She wants to make T_3 > 4. T_3 = x_1^2+x_2^2+x_3^2 = 2 + x_3^2. So she needs x_3^2 > 2 => x_3 > √2≈1.414. So if she plays x_3=1.5, then T_3=2+2.25=4.25>4, so Bazza loses on turn 4. But can she play x_3=1.5? She needs S_3 ≤ 2.4. S_3 = S_2 + x_3 = 1.414+1.5=2.914 ≤2.4? No, 2.914 >2.4, not allowed. So she cannot play 1.5 because that would make the sum exceed λ*3=2.4. She can only play up to 0.986. So x_3 ≤ 0.986. Then x_3^2 ≤ 0.972, so T_3 ≤ 2.972 <4. So she cannot win on turn 4. She could try to win later. Let's simulate with both playing optimally: Alice plays maximum on her turns, Bazza plays maximum on his turns. For λ=0.8, we can compute the sequence. Let's define s_n = sum after n moves, t_n = sum of squares after n moves. Start: s_0=0, t_0=0.

Turn 1 (Alice): choose x1 = λ*1 = 0.8 (max). Then s1=0.8, t1=0.64.

Turn 2 (Bazza): choose x2 = √(2 - t1) = √(2-0.64)=√1.36=1.1662. Then s2=0.8+1.1662=1.9662, t2=0.64+1.36=2.0.

Turn 3 (Alice): choose x3 = λ*3 - s2 = 2.4 - 1.9662 = 0.4338. Then s3=2.4, t3=2.0+0.1882=2.1882.

Turn 4 (Bazza): choose x4 = √(4 - t3) = √(4-2.1882)=√1.8118=1.346. Then s4=2.4+1.346=3.746, t4=2.1882+1.8118=4.0.

Turn 5 (Alice): choose x5 = λ*5 - s4 = 4.0 - 3.746 = 0.254. Then s5=4.0, t5=4.0+0.0645=4.0645.

Turn 6 (Bazza): choose x6 = √(6 - t5) = √(6-4.0645)=√1.9355=1.391. Then s6=4.0+1.391=5.391, t6=4.0645+1.9355=6.0.

Turn 7 (Alice): choose x7 = λ*7 - s6 = 5.6 - 5.391 = 0.209. Then s7=5.6, t7=6.0+0.0437=6.0437.

Turn 9 (Alice): choose x9 = λ*9 - s8 = 6.72 - 6.998 = -0.278, not allowed. So on turn 9, Alice cannot move because s8=6.998 > 6.72. So she loses. This simulation suggests that for λ=0.8, if both play optimally, Bazza wins after 8 turns. But is this strategy optimal for Bazza? He played x2 = √(2 - t1) which is the maximum allowed given t1. That seems to maximize his sum. In the simulation, after his move, t2=2.0, which is exactly 2, so he used the full budget. Then on turn 4, he played x4 = √(4 - t3) with t3=2.1882, so x4=1.346, and t4=4.0, using full budget. So his strategy is to always use the full square budget on his turns. Then Alice on her turns plays the maximum allowed sum. The result is that after his moves, t_n = n (even) for n=2,4,6,8. So he always reaches the maximum allowed sum of squares. This is a natural strategy: on each even turn, he chooses x_n as large as possible, i.e., x_n = √(n - T_{n-1}). This ensures that after his move, T_n = n. Then on Alice's next odd turn, she must choose x_{n+1} such that S_{n+1} ≤ λ(n+1). Since S_n is known, she will choose the maximum possible, i.e., x_{n+1} = λ(n+1) - S_n. This is a deterministic sequence. We can compute the evolution of S_n and T_n. For λ=0.8, we got that at n=8, S_8=6.998, and λ*9=6.72, so S_8 > 6.72, and Alice loses on turn 9. So Bazza wins. So we have a deterministic sequence defined by:
Let s_0 = 0, t_0 = 0.
For n = 1,3,5,... (odd): x_n = λ n - s_{n-1}, so s_n = λ n, t_n = t_{n-1} + (λ n - s_{n-1})^2.

That seems unlikely; the sequence is deterministic and may have a fixed point. Actually, the recurrence might converge to a certain ratio. We can analyze the dynamics.

Let's define for large k, we have s_{2k} ≈ something. From the recurrence, after his move, t_{2k}=2k, and after her move, s_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1). Then the next even move: x_{2k+2} = √(2k+2 - t_{2k+1}). And t_{2k+1} = t_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - s_{2k})^2 = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - s_{2k})^2. So we can compute s_{2k+2} = s_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + √(2k+2 - (2k + (λ(2k+1) - s_{2k})^2)) = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1) - s_{2k})^2). So we have a recurrence for the sum after even moves. Let d_k = λ(2k+1) - s_{2k}. Then s_{2k} = λ(2k+1) - d_k. Then s_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - d_k^2). Then λ(2k+3) - s_{2k+2} = λ(2k+3) - (λ(2k+1) + √(2 - d_k^2)) = 2λ + (λ(2k+3) - λ(2k+1)? Actually λ(2k+3) = λ(2k+1) + 2λ, so difference = 2λ - √(2 - d_k^2). So d_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - d_k^2). This is a recurrence for d_k.

Similarly, we can compute s_{2k} in terms of d_k. The condition for Alice to survive on turn 2k+1 is that s_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). Since s_{2k} = λ(2k+1) - d_k, this is equivalent to d_k ≥ 0. So as long as d_k ≥ 0, she can survive. If d_k becomes negative, she loses. The recurrence for d_k is d_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - d_k^2). Starting from d_0 = λ(1) - s_0 = λ - 0 = λ (since after turn 0, s_0=0, and the bound for turn 1 is λ*1, and s_0=0, so d_0 = λ*1 - s_0 = λ). Squaring: 2 - d^2 = (2λ - d)^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λ d + d^2 => 2 - d^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λ d + d^2 => 2 = 4λ^2 - 4λ d + 2d^2 => divide 2: 1 = 2λ^2 - 2λ d + d^2 => d^2 - 2λ d + 2λ^2 - 1 = 0. This quadratic in d: d = [2λ ± √(4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2-1))]/2 = [2λ ± √(4λ^2 - 8λ^2 + 4)]/2 = [2λ ± √(4 - 4λ^2)]/2 = [2λ ± 2√(1-λ^2)]/2 = λ ± √(1-λ^2). Since d ≥ 0, we have d = λ + √(1-λ^2) or d = λ - √(1-λ^2). The latter is smaller. But also we must have d = 2λ - √(2-d^2). For a given λ, which fixed point is feasible? Given the complexity, perhaps we can find a simpler invariant. Note that the game is symmetric in a certain sense: The constraints are linear in the sum and quadratic in the sum of squares. There is a known inequality: For any nonnegative numbers, (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2. So the sum of squares is at least (∑ x_i)^2 / n. This means that if Alice's sum is S_n, then the sum of squares T_n is at least S_n^2 / n. Conversely, if Bazza's sum of squares is T_n, then the sum S_n is at most √(n T_n). But the game uses these to bound each turn.

Consider the "potential" function V_n = S_n^2 - n T_n? Not sure.

Another approach: The game can be seen as a "duel" where each player can control a resource. There is known problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game where on odd turns Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that ∑_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ nλ, and on even turns Bazza chooses x_n such that ∑ x_i^2 ≤ n. Determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy." I think I've seen a similar problem in IMO Shortlist 2006 or something. Let's analyze the condition for Alice to have a winning strategy. It seems that she can win for any λ > 0.9? But we need to find the exact supremum of λ for which Bazza can win. Our calculations suggest that for λ=0.9, Bazza wins (we saw that with greedy, he won on turn 9). But we should verify if Alice can use a different strategy to win for λ=0.9. Perhaps she could play x1=0.2, etc., but we tested a few and she lost. Could she win by playing x1=0? Then we saw that the greedy response leads to a loss. Could she deviate on turn 3 to play a smaller x3? That would reduce T3, but then maybe she could play a larger x5 later. Let's try to compute the "value" of the game. Possibly we can use a "potential" function that is the "gap" between the sum and the bound. The recurrence we derived for d_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} under the assumption that Alice always plays the maximum sum on her turns. But if she deviates, she might be able to make d_k larger later. However, Bazza will try to minimize her chance of winning. Perhaps we can consider the game from a "strategy stealing" perspective: For Alice to have a winning strategy, there must exist a sequence of moves such that at some odd turn n, she can make T_n > n+1. This is like a "budget" problem. The worst-case for Alice is that Bazza always uses the maximum squares to minimize her winning chances. So we can assume that Bazza plays optimally to win. Then we need to see if there is a strategy for Alice that ensures that eventually T_n > n+1. This is like a "game" where each player can add numbers, and we can compute the "critical" λ for which the game is "balanced".

Given the pattern, it seems that for λ below some threshold, Bazza can force a win by playing greedily (max squares). For λ above that threshold, Alice can win by playing x1=0 and then playing the maximum on each odd turn, which eventually makes T_n exceed the bound. Another idea: The game can be seen as a "duel" where the players alternately add numbers, and the constraints are linear and quadratic. There is a known technique: define a "potential" P = S^2 - T. But we have different bounds for odd and even turns.

Let's think in terms of the "critical" sequence. For a given λ, suppose Alice plays x1 = a. Then Bazza will choose x2 to minimize her chance of winning.
